The choice of rings here additionally allows for an efficiently-computable isomorphism between the polynomial ring and an integer ring, which enables a more efficient pointwise implementation of the multiplication. This transformation is called the Number Theoretic Transform (NTT) and is described further in Section 2.2.1.3. 2.1.6 Module lattices While the ring variants RLWE and RSIS provide useful practical improve-ments in terms of efficiency over their standard counterparts LWE and SIS, they also impose strict requirements on the parameters n and q. Module lattices offer a natural extension to ring lattices, while offering designers of cryptographic schemes additional parameters and thus greater flexibility. A module generalises the concept of a vector space by replacing the underlying field with a ring. Concretely, this means replacing the vectors of polynomials used in the RLWE and RSIS problems with matrices of polynomials to get the 12 | Background following module variants of the LWE and SIS problems. The definitions here are based on those in [27]. Definition 7 (Module Learning with Errors (MLWE)) Let A ∈ Rm×d q be uniformly and randomly chosen, s ∈ Rd q , and e ∈ Rm q be chosen in accordance to a probability distributionχ. Given only the matrix A and a vector t = As+e, find s. Definition 8 (Module Short Integer Solution (MSIS)) Given uniformly and randomly chosen A ∈ Rd×m q and a bound β, find a nonzero vector z ∈ Rm{0} such that Az ≡ 0 mod q and ∥z∥ ≤ β. It is generally assumed that, like the ring variants before, the module variants MLWE and MSIS remain computationally hard, even when using short secrets and errors [27, 31]. 2.1.7 Lattice-based digital signatures Based on the previously described problems, it is now possible to design a lattice-based signature scheme. The core building block is an interactive commit-challenge-response identification protocol, in which the signer commits to a value, the verifier issues a challenge for the commitment and the signer computes the response to the challenge. This interactive protocol is adapted using the Fiat-Shamir transform [32] to be non-interactive by deriving the challenge using a hash function. In a lattice-based signature scheme, the signer cannot respond to certain challenges, thus requiring the signing to be aborted to pick a different challenge. This approach, aptly named “Fiat-Shamir with Aborts”, was first introduced by Lyubashevsky [33, 34]. The following definition is based on those in [34, 35]. Let Dk be the subset of Rq where each polynomial has coefficients in the range [0, . . . , 2k]. Let D1,κ be the subset of polynomials in D1 where at most κ coefficients are nonzero. Let H : {0, 1}∗ → D1,κ be a hash function. Key generation: Let a ∈ Rq. Choose the secret key polynomials s1, s2 ∈ D1 uniformly at random. Compute t = as1 + s2. The public key consists of (a, t). Signing: Let µ be a message to sign. Pick y1, y2 ∈ Dk uniformly at random. Compute challenge c = H(ay1 + y2, µ) and responses z1 = cs1 + y1, z2 = cs2 + y2. Reject the responses if z1 ̸∈ Dk−κ or z2 ̸∈ Dk−κ and retry with different y1, y2. The signature σ consists of (z1, z2, c). Background | 13 Verification: Let σ = (z1, z2, c) be a signature to verify. Compute c′ = H(az1 + z2 − tc, µ). Accept if c = c′ and z1, z2 ∈ Dk−κ. The rejection is necessary to ensure that no information about the secret key is leaked from the responses z1 and z2, which are otherwise related to the secret key polynomials. This is the reason the signer cannot respond to certain challenges, as mentioned earlier. Possible choices of values for parameters k and κ are explored in [35]. Naturally, this scheme can also be extended to module lattices, as is the case in ML-DSA. The scheme used in ML-DSA differs from the scheme described here in its use of additional optimisations. These optimisations are described in Section 2.2.1. 2.2 ML-DSA algorithm Table 2.1: ML-DSA parameter sets from [4]. Parameter set n q d τ γ1 γ2 (k, l) η β ω ML-DSA-44 256 8380417 13 39 217 (q − 1)/88 (4, 4) 2 78 80 ML-DSA-65 256 8380417 13 49 219 (q − 1)/32 (6, 5) 4 196 55 ML-DSA-87 256 8380417 13 60 219 (q − 1)/32 (8, 7) 2 120 75 ML-DSA is derived from the latest version of CRYSTALS-Dilithium [2], and differs in an increased length for parameter ĉ in parameter sets ML-DSA-65 and ML-DSA-87, an increased length of parameter tr, and the introduction of a “hedged” pseudorandom sampling procedure for the private seed ρ′ that replaces the deterministic sampling procedure as the default. A modified variant of the deterministic sampling procedure is, however, retained for ML-DSA, while the previously present fully-randomised sampling method has been removed entirely. An overview over the possible sets of parameters is given in Table 2.1. Additional details and descriptions can be found in the specification [4]. This work will be focusing on ML-DSA-44 (respective Dilithium-2), though other variations can be approached similarly. 14 | Background 2.2.1 Optimisations ML-DSA applies a number of optimisations to the digital signature scheme described in section 2.1.7. These optimisations focus on reducing the size of the generated signatures and speeding up computations. 2.2.1.1 Seed-based sampling The first optimisation applied by ML-DSA reduces the size of randomly sampled matrices and vectors. Instead of storing or sampling matrices/vectors directly, ML-DSA samples a random seed and expands the seed using an extendable output function to sample the entries of the matrices/vectors. For matrices/vectors that must be stored, this reduces the size to that of the seed and for matrices/vectors that must be sampled, this reduces the potentially expensive invocations of a random number generator to those necessary to generate the seed. The design of the extendable output function used in ML-DSA plays a central role in our attack and is described further in section 2.3. 2.2.1.2 Compression The second optimisation reduces the size of the generated signature by omitting the response z2. This technique is based on those described by Güneysu et al. [35] and Bai and Galbraith [36] and uses the insight that part of the response (i.e. the vector z2) mostly carries information about the error. As demonstrating knowledge of the error is not necessary to guarantee the security of the scheme, this information can be omitted by computing the commitment hash c using only the higher-order bits of the value az1 + z2. When the value of z2 is short, the higher-order bits will be identical and thus the resulting signature can be verified correctly. Additionally, it is possible to omit the lower-order bits of the public key t, for a similar reason [2]. As we omit higher-order bits from the value az1+z2− tc, the lower-order bits of t have limited effect on the resulting value. Instead, it is possible to determine the carries that addition of tc causes and only include those carry bits (called a hint) as part of the response, which allows the lower-order bits to be omitted from the public key, thereby reducing its size. 2.2.1.3 Number Theoretic Transform Finally, as mentioned in Section 2.1.5, the chosen representation of the underlying ring allows for a more efficient implementation of the multiplication through the NTT. Background | 15 The NTT is a special case of the Discrete Fourier Transform (DFT) where the underlying field is finite and was first introduced by Pollard [37]. The following definitions are based on those in [38]. Definition 9 ω is a primitive n-th root of unity in Zq if ωn ≡ 1 mod q and ωk ̸≡ 1 mod q for k < n. Definition 10 Let ω be an n-th primitive root of unity. ψ is a primitive 2n-th root of unity in Zq if ψ2 ≡ ω mod q and ψn ≡ −1 mod q. In ML-DSA, we have ψ := 1753. Using these definitions, we can define the transformation as follows. Definition 11 (Number Theoretic Transform (NTT)) Let a ∈ Rq. The transformation that computes the tuple â ∈ Tq with entries âj = n−1∑ i=0 ψ(2j+1)iai mod q for j ∈ {0, . . . , n − 1} is called the Number Theoretic Transform NTT and â is called the representation of a in NTT domain. Definition 12 Let â ∈ Tq. The transformation that computes the vector a with entries ai = n−1 n−1∑ j=0 ψ(2i+1)j âj mod q for i ∈ {0, . . . , n − 1} is called the Inverse Number Theoretic Transform NTT−1. Naturally, we have NTT−1(NTT(a)) = a for a ∈ Rq. The advantage of this approach is that it is possible to compute the product a · b = NTT−1(NTT(a) NTT(b)) via pointwise multiplication in Tq, which is more efficient. 2.2.2 Algorithms The main components of the ML-DSA scheme are the key generation procedure, the signing procedure and the verification procedure. The definitions of the additional utility functions not included here can be found in the specification [4]. 16 | Background Algorithm 1 ML-DSA.KeyGen() [2, 4] Output: Public key pk, private key sk 1: ξ ← {0, 1}256 2: (ρ, ρ′, K) ∈ {0, 1}256 × {0, 1}512 × {0, 1}256 ← H(ξ, 1024) 3: Â← ExpandA(ρ) ▷ Opt: Seed-based sampling 4: (s1, s2)← ExpandS(ρ′) ▷ Opt: Seed-based sampling 5: t← NTT−1(Â NTT(s1)) + s2 6: (t0, t1)← Power2Round(t, d) ▷ Opt: Compression 7: pk ← pkEncode(ρ, t1) 8: tr ← H(BytesToBits(pk), 512) 9: sk ← skEncode(ρ,K, tr, s1, s2, t0) 10: return (pk, sk) 2.2.2.1 Key generation (Algorithm 1) The key generation samples the matrix A and private key polynomial vectors s1 and s2 by generating and expanding a random seed using SHAKE256, see Section 2.2.1.1. The coefficients of s1 and s2 are short, i.e. are in the range [−η, η]. It then computes t = As1 + s2, which forms part of the public key, exactly as in the generic scheme in Section 2.1.7. ML-DSA applies compression (see Section 2.2.1.2) to t by dropping the d least significant bits to reduce its size, but an attacker is assumed to be able to recover the full value of t. The most significant bits t1 and the seed ρ used for expanding the matrix A form the public key. The private key includes the seed ρ, in addition to a private random seedK and the hash tr of the public key for use during signing, as well as vectors s1 and s2 and the d least significant bits t0. 2.2.2.2 Signing (Algorithm 2) The signing procedure computes the message representative µ by hashing the hash of the public key and the message using SHAKE256. The message representative µ corresponds to the message µ from the generic scheme in Section 2.1.7. It then computes an additional private random seed ρ′ by hashing the private random seed K, a 256-bit random value rnd (or in deterministic mode, the value {0}256) and the message representative µ. The seed ρ′ is used to sample the nonce y from which the signer commitment w1 is computed as the high bits of w = Ay. Because ML-DSA applies compression (see Section 2.2.1.2), there is only one response z and thus only one mask y, unlike the pairs of responses and masks in the generic scheme. The commitment hash c̃ is derived from w1 and µ, and is used to sample the Background | 17 Algorithm 2 ML-DSA.Sign(sk,M) [4] Input: Private key sk, message M Output: Signature σ 1: (ρ,K, tr, s1, s2, t0)← skDecode(sk) 2: ŝ1 ← NTT(s1) 3: ŝ2 ← NTT(s2) 4: t̂0 ← NTT(t0) 5: Â← ExpandA(ρ) ▷ Opt: Seed-based sampling 6: µ← H(tr||M, 512) 7: rnd← {0, 1}256 ▷ In deterministic mode: rnd← {0}256 8: ρ′ ← H(K||rnd||µ, 512) 9: κ← 0 10: (z, h)← ⊥ 11: while (z, h) = ⊥ do 12: y← ExpandMask(ρ′, κ) ▷ Opt: Seed-based sampling 13: w← NTT−1(Â NTT(y)) 14: w1 ← HighBits(w) ▷ Opt: Compression 15: c̃ ∈ {0, 1}2λ ← H(µ||w1Encode(w1), 2λ) 16: (c̃1, c̃2) ∈ {0, 1}256 × {0, 1}2λ−256 ← c̃ 17: c← SampleInBall(c̃1) 18: ĉ← NTT(c) 19: cs1 ← NTT−1(ĉ ŝ1) 20: cs2 ← NTT−1(ĉ ŝ2) 21: z← y + cs1 22: r0 ← LowBits(w− cs2) ▷ Opt: Compression 23: if ∥z∥∞ ≥ γ1 − β or ∥r0∥∞ ≥ γ2 − β then (z, h)← ⊥ 24: else 25: ct0 ← NTT−1(ĉ t̂0) 26: h← MakeHint(−ct0,w− cs2 + ct0) ▷ Opt: Compression 27: if ∥ct0∥∞ ≥ γ2 or # of 1’s in h > ω then (z, h)← ⊥ 28: κ← κ+ l 29: σ ← sigEncode(c̃, z mod± q, h) 30: return σ 18 | Background challenge c. This additional step is necessary, as the SHAKE256 hash function used in ML-DSA does not output a polynomial with short coefficients and a limited Hamming weight. Instead, this is realised by the SampleInBall procedure not shown here. The challenge c corresponds to the challenge c in the generic scheme. The signer’s response is calculated as z = y + cs1 and its validity is checked to abort the signing and restart if necessary, exactly as in the generic scheme. Finally, the hint h is computed, which allows a verifier to reconstruct the entire value of w1, as described in Section 2.2.1.2. To reiterate, there are two optimisations happening here simultaneously: First, the second response z2 from the generic scheme is replaced by the value r0, which does not need to be included in the signature at all. Second, to allow the size of the public key to be reduced by omitting its low bits, some of the space saved by omitting the response z2 is reused for the hint h. The final signature σ then consists of the values c̃, z and h. The value c̃ is used instead of the expanded form c, as it has a smaller size. Algorithm 3 ML-DSA.Verify(pk,M, σ) [2, 4] Input: Public key pk, message M , signature σ Output: Boolean 1: (ρ, t1)← pkDecode(pk) 2: (c̃, z, h)← sigDecode(σ) 3: if h = ⊥ then return false 4: Â← ExpandA(ρ) ▷ Opt: Seed-based sampling 5: tr ← H(BytesToBits(pk), 512) 6: µ← H(tr||M, 512) 7: (c̃1, c̃2 ∈ {0, 1}256 × {0, 1}2λ−256 ← c̃ 8: c← SampleInBall(c̃1) 9: w′ Approx ← NTT(Â NTT(z)− NTT(c) NTT(t1 · 2d)) 10: w′ 1 ← UseHint(h,w′ Approx) ▷ Opt: Compression 11: c̃′ ← H(µ||w1Encode(w′ 1), 2λ) 12: return J∥z∥∞ < γ1 − βK and Jc̃ = c̃′K and J # of 1’s in h ≤ ωK 2.2.2.3 Verification (Algorithm 3) The verification procedure first derives the challenge c from the value c̃ using the SampleInBall procedure, thereby undoing the size optimisation of transmitting c̃. It then computes w′ Approx = Ay − ct1 · 2d. Using the hint h to undo the compression, the value w′ 1 is reconstructed from w′ Approx and the commitment hash c̃′ is derived. The verification passes if the Background | 19 commitment hashes match and the additional validity criteria are fulfilled. This matches the generic scheme where the commitment hashes c̃ and c̃′ fulfill the role of the challenges c and c′. As the challenges in ML-DSA are derived deterministically using the SampleInBall procedure, comparing the commitment hashes is equivalent to comparing the challenges derived from them, but allows the last SampleInBall procedure for c̃′ to be omitted. 2.3 SHAKE256 algorithm ML-DSA uses SHAKE256 for pseudorandom sampling of several matrices, vectors and seeds, making it a candidate for side-channel attacks. SHAKE256 is an extendable output function based on KECCAK that was standardised by NIST as part of the Secure Hash Algorithm-3 (SHA-3) standard [39]. Extendable output functions allow arbitrarily-long pseudorandom output to be generated from a given input. KECCAK employs a so-called sponge construction [40] alongside an iterated permutation function KECCAK-f to realise a function with arbitrary output length. As this construction is a central component of the attack in this work, the next paragraph will provide a high-level overview of sponge constructions. A more detailed description of the KECCAK construction is provided in [41]. 0 0 pad ⌊·⌋l f f f f f f r c absorbing squeezing M Z Figure 2.3: Sponge construction (adapted from [40]). Figure 2.3 shows an example of a sponge construction and its two phases. The construction is characterised by its rate r, its capacity c and the choice of the one-way permutation function f . It consists of two buffers of size r and c that form the state of the function, realised as an array of bits. Before 20 | Background output can be generated from the construction during the squeezing phase, the input must first be absorbed during the absorption phase. The input message M is run through a padding function to extend its length to be a multiple of the block size r. Then, a block of the input is XORed with the rate buffer and the resulting state is run through the one-way permutation function f to determine the new state. This is repeated until the input has been exhausted. To generate the output Z as a sequence of blocks of length l, the state is run through the one-way permutation function again and a block is extracted from the rate buffer. This is repeated until the required number of blocks have been generated. 2.4 Fault injection Fault injection attacks are active side-channel attacks that aim to affect the computation performed by a device. In the context of attacks on digital signature algorithms, such faults may be used to allow attackers to extract secret keys or to cause invalid signatures to pass verification. Among the different techniques for fault injection, four main techniques for performing low-cost, minimally invasive fault injection can be identified in the form of software-based glitching, EM glitching, clock glitching and voltage glitching [42, 43]. 2.4.1 Software-based glitching Software-based glitching exploits properties of the executing hardware available through software. The various approaches differ from each other substantially, but include, for example, use of the Dynamic Voltage and Frequency Scaling feature to induce byte-corruption faults through overclocking, as in the CLKscrew attack [44], or bit flips caused by repeated row access in a DRAM chip, as in the RowHammer attacks [45]. 2.4.2 Electromagnetic glitching Electromagnetic (EM) glitching involves precise placement of a probe from which an electromagnetic pulse is emitted. This pulse can affect clock signals or inject additional power into the chip [7]. Several fault injection attacks on Dilithium [10, 46, 47] employ EM glitching, making use of both its ability to skip instructions by affecting the clock and its ability to flip/zero bits in memory. Background | 21 2.4.3 Clock glitching Clock glitching exploits manipulation of the clock signal by injecting or withholding rising edges, thus altering the execution of instructions [7, 43]. Clock glitching has been employed in several attacks on Dilithium [9, 11, 12, 48], which make use of its ability to skip instructions. 2.4.4 Voltage glitching Voltage glitching uses manipulation of the power to a processor to cause faults. Some approaches [49, 50] use uniform underpowering to slow down logic gates to cause faults. These approaches do not require precise timing control, but also offer limited control over affected instructions and the resulting fault. Other approaches [43, 51] instead use a precisely timed spike in the voltage to cause faults. While these approaches thus require greater timing control, they also allow specific instructions to be affected. Device clock Glitched voltage offsetTrigger widthext_offset Figure 2.4: Glitch parameters for ChipWhisperer voltage glitching. Parameters ext_offset and width are expressed in terms of full clock cycles, while offset depends on the internal frequency. This work uses the voltage fault injection technique of O’Flynn [43]. This technique uses a crowbar circuit to short the power rails of the processor, which induces oscillations in the target circuit, thus causing faults. A visualisation of the different parameters of the glitch is shown in Figure 2.4. The parameter ext_offset describes the number of clock cycles between the trigger signal and the beginning of the glitch. The parameter offset describes the beginning of the glitch relative to the rising edge of its first clock cycle. The glitch can begin up to half a clock cycle before the rising edge or up to half a clock cycle after the rising edge. The parameter width describes the number of clock cycles for which the voltage is dropped. 22 | Background 2.4.5 Fault model Using the previously described voltage fault injection technique, the resulting faults will include both (single/multiple) instruction skipping and instruction corruption. While the attack in this work does not make use of instruction corruption, we empirically observed instances of corrupted instructions during the experiments. 2.5 Previous work This section provides an overview of previous physical attacks on CRYSTALS-Dilithium making use of fault injection or side-channel analysis. Bindel et al. [52] presented a number of fault injection attacks against lattice-based signature schemes. One of their approaches is a randomisation attack changing individual coefficients of s1, allowing for the recovery from multiple signatures. They also propose skipping the addition during the computation of z = y+cs1, thus allowing for the recovery of s1 from the same signature. However, their attacks do not target Dilithium and have not been experimentally verified against it. It therefore remains unclear how applicable their approaches are in practice. In a subsequent work, Ravi et al. [8] demonstrated that skipping the entire addition of y is not necessary. Instead, they presented a fault injection attack targeting the addition of single coefficients. This allows for recovery of the full vector s1 in around 1–2k faulty signatures (corresponding to the same number of traces, as they report a fault probability of 100%). Recently, Krahmer et al. [9] extended this addition-skipping attack to randomised Dilithium and additionally presented an attack targeting the matrix A. As both approaches perturb single coefficients in the computation of z = y + cs1 in the signing procedure, they are able to use the verification to exhaustively search and correct the perturbation, thus allowing for recovery of a single coefficient. They practically demonstrate recovery of the full vector s1 using clock glitching in 22952 traces. Separately, Ravi et al. [10] proposed a fault attack exploiting zeroisation of twiddle constants in the NTT. They presented two different attacks targeting deterministic and randomised Dilithium. The first zeroises the NTT of c, thus allowing for recovery of y, which can be used to extract s1 from a different signature. The second attack requires computation of the signature in the NTT domain and zeroises most of the coefficients of y, directly allowing recovery Background | 23 from the signature. They report recovery of the full secret key vector s1 from 13 respective 3 faulted signatures. While they do not report the number of traces required to gather the necessary signatures, they report a fault probability of 26% respective 51% for the attacks. Espitau et al. [53] proposed a fault injection attack targeting the generation of y in a number of Fiat-Shamir with Aborts-based signature schemes. Their approach works by aborting the loop that performs the sampling of y, thus leaving a number of coefficients uninitialised. A signature generated from such a faulty vector will likely directly contain several of the coefficients of the product cs1, allowing for the recovery of the secret key vector. This approach was extended by Ulitzsch et al. [11] to an implementation of Dilithium with fault countermeasures. They show that using an Integer Linear Program, they are able to recover the full vector s1 using 5 faulted signatures gathered from 53 traces using clock glitching. Bruinderink and Pessl [12] demonstrated a differential fault attack against the deterministic version of Dilithium. After generating a signature z for a message, they use fault injection to induce nonce-reuse to obtain a second signature z′ computed for the same y, but a different c. The secret key vector can then be recovered from the difference of the signatures z − z′. While they do not report the number of traces necessary for exploitation, their attack works with faults anywhere in a large range of the execution, thus making it very likely the faulty signature could be obtained from a single attempt, which allows for recovery of the full key s1 from only two signatures. Bauer and De Santis [54] showed a single-fault attack targeting the verification procedure of Dilithium (and another NIST PQC candidate Falcon). The attack works by first submitting a specifically crafted signature for a message and then skipping the subtraction of ct1 during the verification procedure using fault injection, thus causing the device to accept the signature as valid. The attack only requires a single fault to skip the subtraction, though they do not conduct experimental evaluation of their attack nor report the success rate for the fault. There have also been several side-channel attacks on Dilithium that target recovery of the secret key [55–63]. These attacks are generally characterised by a larger number of traces required for successful key recovery. One noteworthy exception is the work by Wang et al. [64] who demonstrated recovery of the secret key vector s1 in a single trace through side-channel power analysis. They exploit leakage of the coefficients of secret key vectors s1 and s2 during the unpacking of the secret key at the beginning of the signing procedure, thus their attack is also applicable to implementations using the 24 | Background hedged mode. By recovering half of these coefficients, they are able to solve a system of linear equations that enables recovery of the full vector s1 with probability of 9% from a single trace. A second exception is the work by Qiao et al. [65], who introduced a method for recovering the secret key vector s1 from the polynomial addition z = y + cs1, which is also applicable to implementations using the hedged mode. They employ a linear regression-based profiled attack to recover the value y from the polynomial unpacking procedure and a CNN-based attack to recover the value cs1 from the Montgomery reduction. By combining the leaked values, they are able to utilise an Integer Linear Program to recover the secret key vector s1 with probability 20% from a single trace. Adversary model and attack scenario | 25 Chapter 3 Adversary model and attack scenario 3.1 Adversary model This section defines assumptions about the adversary, their capabilities and goals in accordance with the adversary model in [66]. 3.1.1 Assumptions It is assumed that the adversary has physical access to the device under attack and access to equipment that allows for fault injection to be performed during the execution of the signing procedure. It is further assumed that the adversary is an outsider without access to privileged information that has a detailed understanding of the implementation of ML-DSA used on the device under attack (e.g. from reverse-engineering). 3.1.2 Capabilities The adversary is capable of detecting the execution of the signing procedure on the device under attack and injecting a precise voltage fault during the execution of the procedure, as well as observing its output. Note that the adversary does not have to be able to control the message being signed, only detecting the signing and observing its output are necessary. To identify parameters for the fault injection, the attacker may use a profiling device to allow them to trigger the signing procedure and observe the internal state after a fault injection attempt. 26 | Adversary model and attack scenario 3.1.3 Goals The goal of the adversary is to perform existential forgery, i.e. to generate a signature σ for an adversary-chosen message M that passes verification using the public key of the device under attack. It is known that knowledge of the secret key vector s1 is sufficient to achieve existential forgery [8]. In the context of firmware signing, existential forgery would allow the adversary to create their own signed firmware that passes verification with the public key used for legitimate firmware updates. Note that only existential forgery (where the adversary picks the message to sign) can be achieved with this approach, not universal forgery (where the message to sign may be picked by a third party), as the signing process can occasionally fail. In the context of firmware signing, this distinction is not important, as it is possible to create bitwise-different firmware versions that are functionally equivalent (for example by introducing NOPs). If the forging process fails, an adversary could thus just attempt to sign a functionally equivalent firmware instead, until they succeed. 3.2 Attack scenario Signer/device-under-attack Attacker (pk, sk) = ML-DSA.KeyGen() Public key pk ML-DSA.Sign(sk,M ) 1: µ← H(tr||M, 512) 2: ρ′ ← . . . 3: σ ← . . . ▷ Compute signature 4: if ML-DSA.Verify(pk,M, σ) then 5: return σ Signature σ fault Figure 3.1: Attack scenario. Figure 3.1 gives an overview of the steps involved in the attack scenario. The attacker acquires the public key of the signer and then triggers or detects the execution of the ML-DSA.Sign procedure on the device under attack. During the computation of the private random seed ρ′, a fault is injected to fix its value to a known constant. The attacker then acquires the generated signature σ and derives the secret key vector s1 from it. Note that fixing the value of ρ′ does not cause the generated signature to be invalid, thus circumventing any countermeasure that checks the validity of the signature after signing, such as those proposed by [48, 52]. Experimental setup | 27 Chapter 4 Experimental setup This chapter describes the equipment used for the fault injection, as well as the target software implementation of ML-DSA. 4.1 Equipment For the experiments, a ChipWhisperer-Husky, a CW313 adapter board and a CW308T-STM32F4 target device are used (see Figure 4.1). The target device contains an ARM Cortex-M4-based STM32F415RGT6, which is run at a frequency of 16 MHz. To avoid having to make any changes to the source code to trigger the fault injection, ARM CoreSight ETM/DWT watchpoints are used. In a real attack scenario, alternative trigger sources such as reference waveforms of the power consumption or communication of the processor with peripheral devices can be used. 4.2 Target implementation In the experiments, a modified version of the CRYSTALS-Dilithium implementation by Abdulrahman et al. [67] is used, in which we added the hedged mode for sampling ρ′ to the crypto_sign_signature procedure, as shown in Listing 4.1. We believe this implementation to be representative for other implementations of the hedged mode, as it follows directly from the specification. Note that the attack targets the implementation of the shake256 procedure, which has not been modified. The implementation is compiled using arm-none-eabi-gcc with the highest optimization level -O3 (recommended default). 28 | Experimental setup Figure 4.1: ChipWhisperer-Husky, CW313 adapter board and CW308T-STM32F4 board used in the experiments. 1 /* Compute message representative µ ... / 23 #ifdef DILITHIUM_USE_HEDGED_MODE 4 / Hedged rnd← {0, 1}256*/ 5 randombytes(rnd, SEEDBYTES); 6 #else 7 /* Deterministic rnd← {0}256*/ 8 memset(rnd, 0, SEEDBYTES); 9 #endif 10 /* Compute ρ′ ← H(K∥rnd∥µ, 512) / 11 shake256(rhoprime, CRHBYTES, key, 12 2SEEDBYTES + CRHBYTES); 13 14 /* Expand matrix A ... */ Listing 4.1: The modified C code of the signing procedure of the CRYSTALS-Dilithium implementation of [67] with hedged mode added. The SHAKE256 function containing the target for the fault injection is highlighted in green. Attack method | 29 Chapter 5 Attack method This chapter describes the fault injection attack method and the implementa-tion of the SHAKE256 algorithm. 5.1 SHAKE256 algorithm In the implementation of Abdulrahman et al., the SHAKE256 algorithm is implemented using four high-level functions. The keccak_inc_init function zero-initialises the Keccak state (represented as an array of 200 bytes). The keccak_inc_absorb function (see Listing 5.1) absorbs an arbitrary number of input bytes into the sponge. The keccak_inc_finalize function finalises the absorption of data and prepares for the extraction of output by applying a padding. Finally, the keccak_inc_squeeze function extracts an arbitrary number of output bytes from the state. To expand an input value into an output value, these functions are called in the order they are listed here. In case the input values are not stored sequentially in memory, the keccak_inc_absorb function may be called multiple times to read from the different input buffers, though this is not the case for the sampling of ρ′ in our implementation. 5.2 Main idea The attack targets the absorption of data into the sponge during the hash calculation of ρ′. Specifically, a single voltage fault is used to skip the branching to the KeccakF1600_StateXORBytes function (see line 11 of Listing 5.1 and line 5 of Listing 5.2). Note that the loop in lines 3 to 9 of Listing 30 | Attack method 1 size_t keccak_inc_absorb(uint64_t *state, size_t n, 2 uint8_t *m, size_t mlen) { 3 while (mlen + n >= 136) { 4 KeccakF1600_StateXORBytes(state, m, n); 5 mlen -= 136 - n; 6 m += 136 - n; 7 n = 0; 8 KeccakF1600_StatePermute(state); 9 } 10 11 KeccakF1600_StateXORBytes(state, m, n, mlen); 12 return n + mlen; 13 } Listing 5.1: The C code of the keccak_inc_absorb procedure. The function targeted by the fault injection is highlighted in green. 1 ... 2 mov r1, r8 3 mov r3, r4 4 mov r0, r7 5 bl KeccakF1600_StateXORBytes 6 ldr r2, [sp, #208] 7 ldr r3, [sp, #212] 8 ... Listing 5.2: Excerpt of the assembly code of the keccak_inc_absorb procedure. The branch targeted by the fault injection is highlighted in green. 5.1 is never executed, because the message length of 64 bytes for the message K||rnd||µ is less than the 136 bytes required to trigger a permutation. As such, skipping the KeccakF1600_StateXORBytes function is sufficient for the sponge to be left empty. This allows an attacker to predict the output ρ′ of the hashing procedure. 5.3 Secret key recovery This section describes the method used to recover the secret key vector s1 from a successfully faulted signature. Recovering the secret key vector s1 from a signature for a known value of Attack method | 31 Table 5.1: Repetition counts for ML-DSA parameter sets from [4]. Parameter set NIST Security Level Repetitions ML-DSA-44 2 4.25 ML-DSA-65 3 5.1 ML-DSA-87 5 3.85 ρ′ is straightforward. A potential approach is shown in Algorithm 4 and works by reconstructing the commitment hash c̃′ using ρ′ and a guess for the value κ. If the commitment hashes match, the challenge c is reconstructed and the secret key vector s1 can be computed as s1 = (z − y) · c−1, where c−1 is the inverse of c in Tq. Note that it is possible for c to have entries with value 0 in the NTT domain. Those entries are not invertible in Tq and the corresponding entries of s1 in NTT domain thus cannot be determined. Empirically, this is rarely the case (sampling 1M random challenges c, we found 21 tuples, all of which contained exactly one entry with value 0). Indeed, we may postulate that the probability for any one coefficient of c to be 0 in the NTT domain is 1 q . However, despite the NTT maintaining uniformly random distributions throughout the mapping, the value of c is strictly speaking not uniformly and randomly distributed, as its Hamming weight is bounded by τ , so this claim may not hold precisely. Nonetheless, we propose to simply enumerate the possible values of s1 in NTT domain (i.e. enumerate all possible values of the entry in Zq) when encountering a coefficient with value 0. For the sake of simplicity, this enumeration procedure is omitted from Algorithm 4. Note further that the choice of parameters for ML-DSA is such that the expected number of iterations in the enumeration of κ is low (around 4, see Table 5.1), thus this approach is generally efficient. 32 | Attack method Algorithm 4 RecoverSecretKey(pk, σ, ρ′, κmax) 1: (ρ, t1)← pkDecode(pk) 2: Â← ExpandA(ρ) 3: (c̃, z, h)← sigDecode(σ) 4: κ← 0 5: while κ < κmax do 6: y← ExpandMask(ρ′, κ) 7: w← NTT−1(Â NTT(y)) 8: w1 ← HighBits(w) 9: c̃′ ∈ {0, 1}2λ ← H(µ||w1Encode(w1), 2λ) 10: if Jc̃ = c̃′K then 11: (c̃1, c̃2) ∈ {0, 1}256 × {0, 1}2λ−256 ← c̃ 12: c← SampleInBall(c̃1) 13: ĉ← NTT(c) 14: s1 ← NTT−1((NTT(z)− NTT(y)) ĉ−1) 15: return s1 16: κ← κ+ l 17: return ⊥ Results and analysis | 33 Chapter 6 Results and analysis This chapter describes the results of the fault injection attack and subsequent secret key recovery. 6.1 Glitch settings We identified settings that consistently skip the desired function without crashing the device or disrupting other steps of the signature generation by conducting a grid search over the set of parameters offered by the ChipWhisperer-Husky. The results here were achieved by using the ‘enable_only’ mode to insert a glitch lasting five clock cycles using both the high-power and low-power crowbar MOSFETs at an offset of 700 units.∗ Using these settings, we managed to successfully skip execution of the KeccakF1600_StateXORBytes function in 52.8% of 1000 attempts. We believe that it is possible to further increase the success rate of the fault injection through additional optimisation of parameters. Among all attempts, certain parameters never resulted in successful faults. For example, using the ‘glitch_only’ mode, that allows to insert glitches with a specified width less than a full clock cycle, did not prove useful in any of the combinations we tested. Similarly, glitches inserted for less than three clock cycles also did not have any effect. Among the offsets, certain ranges of values can be found that pair well with other parameter choices, with values outsides these ranges again showing no effect. A more thorough characterisations of the faults and analysis of parameters is left for future work. ∗These units are dimensionless and depend on the internal frequency of the ChipWhisperer-Husky, but the offset corresponds to the distance between the rising edge of the clock cycle and the beginning of the glitch. See Figure 2.4 for more details. 34 | Results and analysis 6.2 Secret key recovery We applied Algorithm 4 on the signatures generated during the first phase of the attack. As a guess for ρ′, we use the output of SHAKE256 generated by applying the finalisation step on an empty state, which is a constant value easily derived by an attacker. Note that Algorithm 4 handles cases in which the fault injection was unsuccessful by limiting the number of iterations using the bound κmax, thus no additional processing of the signatures is required. We managed to successfully recover the secret key vector s1 for all 52.8% of cases where the fault injection was successful. We did not encounter the scenario in which c is not invertible for any of these signatures. The computation time required to perform the recovery is negligible (less than 10 seconds for a naive implementation). Discussion | 35 Chapter 7 Discussion This chapter describes countermeasures against the presented attack and discusses the relevance of our experimental results for real-world implemen-tations. 7.1 Countermeasures The presented attack would be infeasible if the individual steps of the Keccak algorithm were inlined into the SHAKE256 routine instead of being separated into multiple subroutines. In fact, because the length of the parameters used in the calculation of ρ′ is fixed, it would even be possible to eliminate control flow operations entirely, though such an implementation may not be practical. Implementations of the SHAKE256 routine should also verify that after absorbing data into the sponge and before squeezing output from the sponge, the state is not empty. If this is not the case, the signing procedure should be aborted, thus offering protection against this particular fault attack. Care should then be taken to ensure that the verification is not itself vulnerable to fault injection attacks. For the hedged mode itself, another alternative would be to randomly initialise the state. The presented fault attack would then be insufficient to recover the value of ρ′. This countermeasure is not applicable to the deterministic mode, as it introduces non-determinism. Additionally it should be noted that the specification of SHAKE256 makes no claims about the properties of the construction with a randomly initialised state. More robust countermeasures would require changes to the signing procedure. One such approach may be to move the computation of ρ′ into the rejection sampling loop. In that case, an attacker would either be required to predict or affect (through e.g. additional fault injection) the number of 36 | Discussion times that the rejection sampling is run to inject a single fault during the computation of a signature that is not rejected, or have to inject faults into multiple iterations. This would increase the complexity of an attack. Given the generally high probability of success of the fault injection in this attack and the choice of parameters in Dilithium that inherently keep the number of iterations in the rejection sampling low (see Table 5.1), it is unclear if this approach would be sufficient to prevent an attack. A different approach is to increase the complexity in recovering the private key after a successful fault injection during the computation of ρ′. Here it may be possible to make the value of κ used during the sampling of y unpredictable (e.g. by increasing its size and initialising it randomly), which would require additional randomness or extension of existing random values. Alternatively, it may be possible to include the attacker-unknown value K (or in hedged mode, rnd or a combination of both) in the sampling of y, as proposed by [12]. This eliminates the single point-of-failure around the computation of ρ′ at the cost of increasing the input size to the hash function during the sampling of y. 7.2 Limitations The ChipWhisperer platform is an idealised target for physical attacks, including fault injection. A number of resistors and filtering components typically present in real devices have been omitted from the ChipWhisperer target devices for the purposes of easing power side-channel and fault injection research, thereby lowering the difficulty of attacks. Additionally, the level of control of the target device exercised in the attack to generate trigger signals with the required precision also represents an ideal scenario for an attacker. The difficulty of finding correct timings for the fault injection on a real device is likely much higher, especially at higher target frequencies. It should also be noted that the attack here is performed on a modified implementation of CRYSTALS-Dilithium and not on an implementation of ML-DSA. While we believe our implementation to be representative of how other implementations will implement the hedged mode, these implementations (and, strictly speaking, the standard they implement) do not yet exist. With further changes to the SHAKE256 Application Programming Interface (API) having been announced for ML-DSA (and the related draft standard ML-KEM) [68], actual implementations may end up looking different. Additionally, actual hardware implementations (as opposed to the purely software-based implementation used in this attack) may make greater use of parallelisation and employ countermeasures against fault injection Discussion | 37 attacks, which can increase the difficulty of successfully carrying out an attack. In fact, previous work [69, 70] has shown that transient faults on power rails (like those used in this work) can be detected with reasonable accuracy. However, the core insight from our attack, namely that the ML-DSA scheme has a single point-of-failure around the computation of the private seed ρ′, is valid regardless of the precise method used to exploit this shortcoming. Furthermore, not all real implementations will make use of available countermeasures and fault injection methods may be adaptable to bypass countermeasures. Therefore the results of this work and the specific countermeasures proposed in the previous section are relevant, even outside an academic context. 38 | Discussion Other attacks | 39 Chapter 8 Other attacks This chapter presents additional attacks on ML-DSA that are not part of the main content of the thesis. This includes a theoretical flaw in the standard draft and a side-channel attack on the hedged mode. 8.1 Breaking strong unforgeability In February of 2024, Mike Hamburg pointed out on the NIST PQC mailing list that the hint unpacking procedure as specified in the ML-DSA draft version may be insufficient to protect against hint reordering attacks, thus breaking SUF-CMA security [71]. We experimentally verified the attack against the unpacking procedure described in the draft and investigated the susceptibility of both the reference implementation and the implementation used in this work. Our analysis confirmed that a trivial reordering of hint bits will indeed generate the same hint from bitwise different packed hints for the unpacking procedure described in the draft, thus breaking SUF-CMA security. However, we further confirmed that existing implementations, which derive from the official reference implementation, are not vulnerable to this attack, as they contain an additional check to prevent hint reordering not present in the ML-DSA draft. In a subsequent comment, NIST confirmed that the additional check will be introduced into the final version of ML-DSA [68, 72]. The following section provides further details on how the hint reordering attack can be carried out and how to prevent it by modifying the hint unpacking procedure. 40 | Other attacks 8.1.1 Hint bit reordering attack Given a signature σ, a forged signature σ′ for the same message can be generated as follows. First extract the bit-packed hint y is extracted as the last ω+ k bytes of σ. This bit-packed hint contains the ω indices of the bits of the hint that are 1, followed by the k end indices of the individual sequences. Thus, the sequence of indices y[0], . . . , y[y[ω]−1] belongs to the first element of the vector, the sequence of indices y[y[ω]], . . . y[y[ω + 1] − 1] belongs to the second element of the vector and so on. Further details on the packing procedure and bit-ordering of hints can be found in the specification [4]. It is then possible to arbitrarily permute the order of indices within these sequences. For example, the elements y[0] and y[1] can be swapped to form a new hint y′ (provided that y[ω] > 1, i.e. the sequence has at least two elements, otherwise we can pick a different sequence). By replacing the original hint y with the modified hint y′ in σ, a forged signature σ′ can be created. Using the hint bit unpacking procedure as specified in the ML-DSA draft, the signatures σ and σ′ will be equivalent, even though they are bitwise different, thus breaking the SUF-CMA security. Algorithm 5 HintBitUnpack(y) (modified from [4]) Input: A byte string y of length ω + k Output: A polynomial vector h ∈ Rk 2 or ⊥ 1: h ∈ Rk 2 ← 0k 2: Index← 0 3: for i from 0 to k − 1 do 4: if y[ω + i] < Index or y[ω + i] > ω then return ⊥ 5: First← Index 6: while Index < y[ω + i] do 7: if Index > First and y[Index] ≤ y[Index− 1] then return ⊥ 8: h[i]y[Index] ← Index + 1 9: Index← Index + 1 10: while Index < ω do 11: if y[Index] ̸= 0 then return ⊥ 12: Index← Index + 1 13: return h Other attacks | 41 8.1.2 Countermeasure Algorithm 5 shows a modified version of the bit unpacking procedure that contains an additional check (highlighted in orange) to ensure that hint indices are only allowed to occur in ascending order. With this modification, the described attack is no longer possible. 42 | Other attacks 8.2 Side-channel leakage during absorption In addition to the fault injection attack on the derivation of the seed ρ′ in the hedged mode, we identified side-channel leakage during the same procedure that could potentially be exploited to extract the secret key in a similar way. This section provides a brief introduction to side-channel analysis, gives an overview of previous side-channel attacks on KECCAK and describes the leakage and potential attack. 8.2.1 Side-channel analysis 8.2.1.1 Side-channels In contrast to active side-channel attacks, like the fault injection technique used in the main part of this work, passive side-channel attacks only observe information about the execution of an algorithm and do not attempt to affect its results. This information is leaked through sources other than the input and output to the algorithm. Such sources are called side-channels and manifest themselves in a variety of ways. The perhaps most well-known types of side-channels are timing side-channels, first introduced by Kocher [73], where differences in the execution time of instructions or steps of an algorithm can provide insights about the data being processed. Similarly, cache side-channels, as used in the Spectre attacks [74], can also be used to extract information by evicting entries from the cache of a processor. Among the physical side-channels, EM side-channels [75] and power side-channels [76] are commonly used and exploit electromagnetic emanations or differences in the power consumption to reveal information. Power side-channel analysis is natively supported by the equipment used in the main part of this work and is thus a convenient choice for the investigation in this section. 8.2.1.2 Test vector leakage assessment and Welch’s t-test To evaluate the effectiveness of countermeasures against non-invasive attacks (like power side-channel analysis), NIST launched a non-invasive attack testing workshop in 2011. As part of the workshop, Goodwill et al. [77] presented a technique now known as Test Vector Leakage Assessment (TVLA). They broadly outline a methodology that works by correlating traces captured from a device-under-test with known test vectors using Welch’s t-test [78]. The t-test provides a statistical measure to describe the similarity of two sets for an observed property. In the case of power side-channel analysis, Other attacks | 43 the observed property is the measured power consumption of the device. The set of traces T is partitioned into groups T0 and T1 based on the assumption that the processing leaks the Hamming weight of the values. For a specific bit in the test vector, the set T0 then contains all traces where the bit was 0 and T1 contains the traces where the bit was 1. For each sample in the trace, the t-value can be determined as follows: t = µ0 − µ1√ σ2 0 |T0| + σ2 1 |T1| (8.1) where µ and σ denote the mean and standard deviation of the samples in T0 and T1. The larger the differences between the two sets are, the higher the absolute value of t will be. Goodwill et al. propose a threshold of ±4.5, that, when exceeded, indicates leakage with high confidence. 8.2.1.3 Deep learning Recent side-channel attacks increasingly make use of machine learning techniques like Deep Learning to extract information from the side-channel. As described in [79], a profiled Deep Learning Differential Power Analysis (DPA) side-channel attack may work as follows: First, an attacker acquires power traces from a profiling device they have full control over. Each trace is labelled with the key that was used by the algorithm to produce it. Then a machine learning model is trained on a section of the traces that contains leakage (such sections can be identified using TVLA). The model learns to distinguish between the power consumption of the device when processing bits with value 0 and with value 1. In a final step, an attacker can use the trained model on traces acquired from the device under attack to predict the bits of the key. 8.2.2 Previous work Kannwischer et al. [80] present a power side-channel attack on KECCAK that makes use of belief-propagation to extract a secret input. They simulate leakage of Hamming weights using estimates gathered by profiling a STM32F405, similar to the device used in this work. Their analysis focuses on leakage of full state stores during the θ, χ and π ρ steps in a KECCAK-f round and explicitly excludes XORing during absorption. Using the leakage, they are able to recover the secret input in a single trace. Taha and Schaumont [81, 82] present two power side-channel attacks 44 | Other attacks on Message Authentication Code (MAC)-KECCAK that take into account the key size. Their first attack makes use of leakage during the XOR between KECCAK-f iterations, the θ step and the χ step, depending on the key size. Their attack is capable of recovering large parts of the secret key in fewer than 15000 traces using Correlation Power Analysis (CPA). Their second attack presents a general procedure for attacking MAC-KECCAK with variable key lengths using iterated DPA. The attack again uses leakage in the θ and χ steps and is capable of recovering large parts of the secret key at higher key lengths in fewer than 35000 traces using CPA. Luo et al. [83] describe a power side-channel attack on an Field-Programmable Gate Array (FPGA)-implementation of MAC-KECCAK. They construct leakage models that are applicable to the parallelised hardware implementation and assume leakage during the θ step of a KECCAK-f round. Using these leakage models with CPA, they are able to recover parts of the key from between 10000 and 500000 traces with high probability. 8.2.3 Leakage Implementations of KECCAK generally operate on 64-bit lanes at a time. On architectures with a word size of 32 bits, like the ARMv7E-M architecture of the Cortex-M4 used in this work, the 64-bit lane must be split into two 32-bit words. The authors of the KECCAK permutation suggest a bit-interleaving technique [84] for such implementations that splits data into even and odd bits and stores these in separate words. This technique is used in the implementation by Abdulrahman et al. [67] used in this work, and requires an additional step to map the input and output to the interleaved representation. Specifically, as part of the absorption into the sponge in the KeccakF1600_StateXORBytes procedure, the implementation uses the toBitInterleaving procedure (see Listing 8.1) to interleave bits of input from registers r4 and r5 into registers r6 (odd bits) and r7 (even bits). A similar step is taken in the fromBitInterleaving procedure during extraction of bytes, but we focus on the absorption for this analysis, as this allows combining information from multiple traces, thus lowering the number of bits that need to be extracted at once for a successful attack. By applying the TVLA technique described in Section 8.2.1.2, we identified leakage of the secret key value K (and the value rnd) used in the computation of ρ′ = H(K||rnd||µ, 512) that is part of the hedged mode. This leakage is shown in Figure 8.1. Analysis of the program counter suggests that this leakage occurs in the toBitInterleaving procedure that is invoked Other attacks | 45 0 5k 10k 15k 20k 25k 30k 35k 40k 0 5 10 15 20 Bit 0 Bit 1 Bit 2 Bit 3 Bit 4 Trace point t -t e s t  s c o r e Figure 8.1: T-test results from 10K traces for leakage of bits 0 to 4 of the input value K during computation of ρ′ = H(K||rnd||µ, 512). once for each of the 5 · 5 lanes in the KECCAK state and there are clear peaks visible in excess of the threshold of±4.5 of Goodwill et al. It has not yet been possible to establish exactly which operations are causing the leakage, though bit shifts and XORs are likely candidates. It is further unclear why the leakage repeats in two distinct segments. Naturally, lanes are accessed multiple times during the absorption if a sufficiently long input is processed, but this should not be the case for the comparatively short input of K||rnd||µ. The leakage also occurs across even and odd bits in the input in both segments, so it does not seem to belong to the processing of different parts of the lane. We leave further investigation of these phenomena as a part of future work. 8.2.4 Attack To investigate whether the leakage identified using TVLA is practically exploitable, we experimentally perform machine learning-based extraction of bits from a set of traces acquired from the device. The dataset contains 10000 traces acquired at an ADC multiplier of 32. Blocks of 10 traces are averaged to reduce the noise and regions of interest of 256 sample points are selected by concatenating together parts of the trace with high t-values for the first 5 bits of the input value K. We use Multilayer perceptron (MLP) neural networks with the architecture shown in Table 8.1. During training, we use the Nadam optimiser with a learning rate of 0.001 and a numerical stability constant ϵ = 10−8. The model is trained for a maximum of 300 epochs using early stopping with a patience of 50 and a batch size of 512, and 70% of the data is used for training and 30% for validation. The trained model is then capable of recovering the first 5 bits of 46 | Other attacks 1 and r3,r4,#0x55555555 2 orr r3,r3,r3, LSR #1 3 and r3,r3,#0x33333333 4 orr r3,r3,r3, LSR #2 5 and r3,r3,#0x0F0F0F0F 6 orr r3,r3,r3, LSR #4 7 and r3,r3,#0x00FF00FF 8 bfi r3,r3,#8, #8 9 eor r6,r6,r3, LSR #8 10 11 and r3,r5,#0x55555555 12 orr r3,r3,r3, LSR #1 13 and r3,r3,#0x33333333 14 orr r3,r3,r3, LSR #2 15 and r3,r3,#0x0F0F0F0F 16 orr r3,r3,r3, LSR #4 17 and r3,r3,#0x00FF00FF 18 orr r3,r3,r3, LSR #8 19 eor r6,r6,r3, LSL #16 20 21 and r3,r4,#0xAAAAAAAA 22 orr r3,r3,r3, LSL #1 23 and r3,r3,#0xCCCCCCCC 24 orr r3,r3,r3, LSL #2 25 and r3,r3,#0xF0F0F0F0 26 orr r3,r3,r3, LSL #4 27 and r3,r3,#0xFF00FF00 28 orr r3,r3,r3, LSL #8 29 eor r7,r7,r3, LSR #16 30 31 and r3,r5,#0xAAAAAAAA 32 orr r3,r3,r3, LSL #1 33 and r3,r3,#0xCCCCCCCC 34 orr r3,r3,r3, LSL #2 35 and r3,r3,#0xF0F0F0F0 36 orr r3,r3,r3, LSL #4 37 and r3,r3,#0xFF00FF00 38 orr r3,r3,r3, LSL #8 39 bfc r3, #0, #16 40 eors r7,r7,r3 Listing 8.1: Assembly code of the toBitInterleaving procedure. Other attacks | 47 Table 8.1: Neural network architecture used for partial key recovery. Layer type Output shape Batch Normalization 1 256 Dense 1 16 ReLU 16 Dense 2 8 ReLU 8 Dense 3 2 Softmax 2 the input value K with an accuracy of 99.83% from the leakage in the toBitInterleaving procedure. To perform an attack capable of recovering the entire key, it would be necessary to recover at least the first 256 + 256 bits of the input K||rnd||µ using this technique. Note that K is constant across multiple invocations of the signing routine, thus it would be possible to combine information from multiple traces. However, all 256 bits of the value rnd would need to be recovered in a single trace, which may be infeasible given the accuracy of the model. Knowledge of K and rnd is sufficient to predict the value of ρ′ for a given message, thus the rest of the key recovery could then be performed exactly as described in Section 5.3. 8.2.5 Conclusions We demonstrated that the toBitInterleaving procedure, used in the KeccakF1600_StateXORBytes procedure as part of the absorption of data into the sponge, leaks information about the secret input values K and rnd. We further demonstrated that it is possible to recover a part of this information using machine learning with high accuracy. However, in order to mount a successful attack capable of recovering the secret key used in the signing procedure of ML-DSA, an attacker would be required to recover a substantially higher number of bits and to potentially do so from a single trace as opposed to the averaged traces we used. It is at this point unclear whether such an attack would be feasible. It should also be pointed out that countermeasures against side-channel attacks have been proposed for KECCAK, including masking and hiding [80, 85, 86]. The implementation used in this work does not employ any such countermeasures, thus it remains unclear whether they make side-channel 48 | Other attacks attacks more difficult or even infeasible. Generally, countermeasures against side-channel attacks for KECCAK have focused on protecting parts of the KECCAK-f permutation and not the absorption procedure targeted in this attack, thus their applicability in this context needs to be investigated further. Conclusions and future work | 49 Chapter 9 Conclusions and future work This section presents conclusions about the presented attack and describes future work for strengthening the security of implementations of PQC algorithms. 9.1 Conclusions We presented a practical fault injection attack on a hedged implementation of ML-DSA. We identified settings that consistently skip the desired function without crashing the devices or disrupting other steps of the signature generation. The attack can be applied to other parameter sets of ML-DSA. Our work demonstrates that it is possible to recover the secret key vector in a single attempt with high probability, with the generated signature passing verification. This highlights the importance of protecting the calculations of the private random seed ρ′, especially when using the hedged mode. Previous work on fault attacks against Dilithium has focused exclusively on the pre-standardisation variant CRYSTALS-Dilithium, while the changes introduced by the ML-DSA variant currently being standardised have not been adequately assessed. Despite the attack presented in this work, we still believe that the hedged mode offers useful protections for implementations against weak and manipulated Random Number Generators (RNGs). It is admittedly unclear at this stage how an attack would be able to exploit a biased RNG used to sample ρ′, as the vector y is later sampled by applying the SHAKE256 function, thus diffusing any biases. However, the hedged mode does prevent trivial zeroisation attacks on the RNG output from immediately disclosing the secret key. Instead, in such cases, the security of the scheme only reduces to 50 | Conclusions and future work that of the deterministic mode (which may then be vulnerable to differential attacks [12]). In addition to the fault injection attack on the seed derivation, we also investigated other attacks on ML-DSA in Chapter 8. The first of these is a reported vulnerability in the draft version of the ML-DSA standard that can be used to break SUF-CMA security. We constructed an attack that uses hint reordering to cause the bit unpacking of the verification procedure to generate the same values from different inputs. We also demonstrated the additional check required for mitigating this vulnerability that will be added to the final version of the ML-DSA standard. The second attack is a side-channel attack that uses leakage during a bit interleaving step of the absorption of data into the KECCAK sponge during the derivation of ρ′. We demonstrated that this procedure in its current implementation reveals information about the secret inputs to the hash function through the power consumption of the device and that it is possible to extract part of this information using machine learning. We further outlined how a potential attack might use this approach to recover the secret key in a similar way to the fault injection attack. 9.2 Future work The attack in this work focuses on a software implementation of ML-DSA adapted from existing CRYSTALS-Dilithium implementations. Future work should extend the analysis to actual implementations of ML-DSA, including hardware implementations, once those become available. Such implementations may employ different implementation strategies and thus exhibit different characteristics for fault injection attacks. Additionally, the strength of existing countermeasures against fault injection and side-channel analysis for KECCAK and SHAKE256 implementations, and the strength of the countermeasures proposed in this work should be analysed. Furthermore, SHAKE256 is an important building block for a number of NIST PQC algorithms, including the closely related ML-KEM and SLH-DSA draft standards. The adaptability of the fault injection attack presented in this attack to those algorithms and the consequences of such an attack should be explored further. Additionally, the recently-announced changes [68] to the extendable output function API used for SHAKE256 in ML-DSA and ML-KEM should be studied. These changes make the structure of function calls that we attacked in this work an explicit part of the standard for certain uses of SHAKE256 with Conclusions and future work | 51 variable output length. While this does not include the sampling of the seed ρ′, which has a fixed length, implementations that directly follow the standard are likely to use the same structure across all uses of SHAKE256, thus leading to vulnerable implementations. In more general terms, it would be beneficial to characterise the injected faults further to try and identify why certain choices of parameters did or did not yield the expected results and whether it is possible to further optimise the success rate of the attack. Similarly, exploring the possibility of attacking hedged ML-DSA in the more commonly used bit-flip fault model (e.g. when using EM fault injection) would also be useful future work, as existing research in this area focuses only on CRYSTALS-Dilithium. Moreover, the additional check necessary to prevent hint reordering from breaking SUF-CMA security demonstrated in Section 8.1.2 and Algorithm 5 is likely vulnerable to fault injection attacks. We deliberately excluded analysis of the verification procedure for fault injection attacks, as we believe that a fault injection attack capable of bypassing specific parts of the verification procedure could also be used to bypass the verification entirely, which has substantially more far-reaching security implications. Nonetheless, improving the understanding of and developing countermeasures for more subtle attacks on the verification procedure would be beneficial for the security of implementations. Finally, the side-channel leakage of the bit interleaving procedure presented in Section 8.2 should be investigated further. This includes establishing whether it is possible to scale the recovery method to extract a sufficiently large number of bits to mount a successful attack and whether existing countermeasures against side-channel analysis are effective against the attack. 52 | Conclusions and future work References | 53 References [1] P.
